{"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01Us8cEdWpquLr6RpovtX5qt","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"tool_use","id":"toolu_01MJLG8u1srbGhF3DCPR76oh","name":"Write","input":{"file_path":"/home/runner/work/calccrunch/calccrunch/src/content/blog/celsius-fahrenheit-explained.md","content":"To convert Celsius to Fahrenheit, multiply by 9/5 (or 1.8) and add 32: **°F = (°C × 9/5) + 32**. For the reverse, subtract 32 then multiply by 5/9: **°C = (°F − 32) × 5/9**. You can plug any number into our free [Celsius to Fahrenheit converter](/calculators/celsius-to-fahrenheit) and get an instant answer — but understanding the formula means you'll never be stuck without a calculator.\n\n## The Exact Formula\n\nThe official conversion formula comes from the relationship between the two scales' fixed reference points:\n\n- Water freezes at **0 °C** = **32 °F**\n- Water boils at **100 °C** = **212 °F**\n\nThe gap of 100 °C spans 180 °F, giving a ratio of 180/100 = **9/5 = 1.8**. That's where the multiplier comes from. The +32 accounts for the offset at the freezing point.\n\n| Direction | Formula | Example |\n|---|---|---|\n| Celsius → Fahrenheit | °F = (°C × 9/5) + 32 | 20 °C → (20 × 1.8) + 32 = **68 °F** |\n| Fahrenheit → Celsius | °C = (°F − 32) × 5/9 | 98.6 °F → (98.6 − 32) × 0.556 = **37 °C** |\n\nUse the [Fahrenheit to Celsius converter](/calculators/fahrenheit-to-celsius) when you're working from Fahrenheit back to Celsius.\n\n## A Fast Mental-Math Shortcut\n\nThe exact formula isn't always practical in your head. Here's a two-step approximation that's accurate to within 1–2 degrees:\n\n**Celsius → Fahrenheit (quick):** Double the Celsius temperature, subtract 10%, then add 32.\n\nExample: 25 °C → 25 × 2 = 50 → 50 − 5 = 45 → 45 + 32 = **77 °F** (exact: 77 °F ✓)\n\n**Fahrenheit → Celsius (quick):** Subtract 32, then halve the result and add 10%.\n\nExample: 95 °F → 95 − 32 = 63 → 63 ÷ 2 = 31.5 → 31.5 + 3.15 ≈ **35 °C** (exact: 35 °C ✓)\n\nFor casual use — checking the weather, cooking, or travel — this shortcut gets you close enough without a calculator.\n\n## Common Temperature Reference Points\n\nMemorizing a handful of landmarks makes any other temperature easy to estimate:\n\n| Celsius | Fahrenheit | What it feels like |\n|---|---|---|\n| −40 °C | −40 °F | The only point where both scales are equal |\n| −18 °C | 0 °F | Extremely cold winter day |\n| 0 °C | 32 °F | Water freezes / ice point |\n| 10 °C | 50 °F | Cool autumn day |\n| 20 °C | 68 °F | Comfortable room temperature |\n| 25 °C | 77 °F | Warm summer day |\n| 37 °C | 98.6 °F | Normal human body temperature |\n| 40 °C | 104 °F | Fever / very hot day |\n| 100 °C | 212 °F | Water boils (at sea level) |\n| 180 °C | 356 °F | Typical baking temperature |\n\nWhen you're traveling abroad and the weather app shows 28 °C, you know from this table it's between 77 °F (25 °C) and 86 °F (30 °C) — a warm summer day, not a heat wave.\n\n## Where Each Scale Is Used\n\n**Celsius** (°C) is the global standard. It's used in:\n- Every country except the United States, Liberia, and the Cayman Islands for everyday weather\n- Scientific and medical contexts worldwide (body temperature, lab work)\n- Cooking in most of the world\n\n**Fahrenheit** (°F) is the dominant scale in:\n- The United States for everyday weather and cooking temperatures\n- Aviation in some countries (airport weather reports in the US)\n- Some older engineering standards\n\n<div class=\"callout\">\nWhen a US recipe calls for a 350 °F oven and you have a Celsius oven, use the formula: (350 − 32) × 5/9 = 176.7 °C. Round to 175 °C or 180 °C depending on your oven's settings.\n</div>\n\n## Celsius to Fahrenheit: A Full Conversion Chart\n\n| °C | °F | °C | °F |\n|---|---|---|---|\n| −30 | −22 | 25 | 77 |\n| −20 | −4 | 30 | 86 |\n| −10 | 14 | 35 | 95 |\n| −5 | 23 | 37 | 98.6 |\n| 0 | 32 | 40 | 104 |\n| 5 | 41 | 50 | 122 |\n| 10 | 50 | 60 | 140 |\n| 15 | 59 | 75 | 167 |\n| 20 | 68 | 100 | 212 |\n\nFor any temperature not in this table, use our [Celsius to Fahrenheit calculator](/calculators/celsius-to-fahrenheit) — just type the number and get the exact conversion instantly.\n\n## Why −40 Is the Same in Both Scales\n\nThe curiosity that the two scales intersect at −40 is not a coincidence — it's built into the math. Set the two formulas equal:\n\n°C = (°C × 9/5) + 32  \n°C − (°C × 9/5) = 32  \n°C × (1 − 9/5) = 32  \n°C × (−4/5) = 32  \n°C = −40\n\nSo at exactly −40, both thermometers read the same number. This is useful trivia, but also a useful sanity check: if you ever see a conversion claiming −40 °C ≠ −40 °F, something went wrong.\n\n## Frequently Asked Questions\n\n**What is 100 °C in Fahrenheit?**\n100 °C = 212 °F. This is the boiling point of water at sea level (1 atm of pressure). At higher altitudes, water boils at lower temperatures because atmospheric pressure is reduced.\n\n**What is 37 °C in Fahrenheit?**\n37 °C = 98.6 °F. This is the standard normal human body temperature, though healthy individuals vary between about 36.1 °C (97 °F) and 37.2 °C (99 °F) throughout the day.\n\n**What is 0 °C in Fahrenheit?**\n0 °C = 32 °F. This is the freezing point of water, and the offset built into the Fahrenheit formula (+32).\n\n**Is there a temperature scale that uses absolute zero?**\nYes — the Kelvin scale (K) starts at absolute zero (−273.15 °C / −459.67 °F). It's used in physics and chemistry. To convert: K = °C + 273.15. There are no negative Kelvin values.\n\n**Which scale is more precise?**\nNeither scale is inherently more precise — both can be measured to as many decimal places as your thermometer allows. The Celsius degree is slightly larger than the Fahrenheit degree (1 °C = 1.8 °F), so Fahrenheit gives finer resolution for everyday temperatures without decimals.\n\n## The Bottom Line\n\nThe Celsius-to-Fahrenheit formula is **(°C × 9/5) + 32**. For a quick mental estimate, double the Celsius number, subtract 10%, and add 32. Bookmark the key reference points — 0 °C = 32 °F, 20 °C = 68 °F, 37 °C = 98.6 °F, 100 °C = 212 °F — and most everyday temperature questions answer themselves.\n\nFor any exact conversion, use the free [Celsius to Fahrenheit calculator](/calculators/celsius-to-fahrenheit) or the [Fahrenheit to Celsius calculator](/calculators/fahrenheit-to-celsius) — no sign-up required.\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"stop_details":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":1,"cache_creation_input_tokens":364,"cache_read_input_tokens":78854,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":364,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0},"output_tokens":64,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"}},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"1c8a1dfc-fb9e-493f-8684-48d48c0fa434","uuid":"55088a88-9e5d-42de-a54b-9716e08054fe"}

This is the freezing point of water, and the offset built into the Fahrenheit formula (+32).\n   106→\n   107→**Is there a temperature scale that uses absolute zero?**\n   108→Yes — the Kelvin scale (K) starts at absolute zero (−273.15 °C / −459.67 °F). It's used in physics and chemistry. To convert: K = °C + 273.15. There are no negative Kelvin values.\n   109→\n   110→**Which scale is more precise?**\n   111→Neither scale is inherently more precise — both can be measured to as many decimal places as your thermometer allows. The Celsius degree is slightly larger than the Fahrenheit degree (1 °C = 1.8 °F), so Fahrenheit gives finer resolution for everyday temperatures without decimals.\n   112→\n   113→## The Bottom Line\n   114→\n   115→The Celsius-to-Fahrenheit formula is **(°C × 9/5) + 32**. For a quick mental estimate, double the Celsius number, subtract 10%, and add 32. Bookmark the key reference points — 0 °C = 32 °F, 20 °C = 68 °F, 37 °C = 98.6 °F, 100 °C = 212 °F — and most everyday temperature questions answer themselves.\n   116→\n   117→For any exact conversion, use the free [Celsius to Fahrenheit calculator](/calculators/celsius-to-fahrenheit) or the [Fahrenheit to Celsius calculator](/calculators/fahrenheit-to-celsius) — no sign-up required.\n   118→"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"1c8a1dfc-fb9e-493f-8684-48d48c0fa434","uuid":"da936377-974e-4007-a46c-60cafe414e9f"}

{"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01DDba4e78fZL92rH1gphkWP","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"tool_use","id":"toolu_01GXnurK7XqLbQ6QTzjMLWMe","name":"Write","input":{"file_path":"/home/runner/work/calccrunch/calccrunch/src/content/blog/salary-vs-hourly-pay.md","content":"Salaried employees earn a fixed annual amount regardless of hours worked. Hourly employees are paid per hour and receive overtime (typically 1.5× their rate) for hours beyond 40 per week. **Which pays more depends on your hours and industry** — a salaried $52,000/year employee working 50 hours a week earns less per hour than a $25/hour worker with steady 40-hour weeks. Use the [salary to hourly calculator](/calculators/salary-to-hourly-calculator) to compare your own numbers before accepting any offer.\n\n## Salary vs Hourly: Side-by-Side Comparison\n\n| Factor | Salaried | Hourly |\n|---|---|---|\n| Pay structure | Fixed annual amount | Per-hour rate |\n| Overtime | Usually none (exempt employees) | Mandatory 1.5× after 40 hrs (non-exempt) |\n| Benefits | More likely: health, 401k, PTO | Less common; often separate budgets |\n| Schedule flexibility | More common | Tied to scheduled shifts |\n| Income predictability | High — same check every period | Varies with hours scheduled |\n| Tax treatment | Standard W-2 withholding | Standard W-2 withholding |\n| Job security | Often (not always) higher | More subject to hour cuts |\n| Career growth | Promotions more structured | Raises often per-review or per-role |\n\n## Converting Between Salary and Hourly\n\nThe standard conversion assumes **2,080 working hours per year** (40 hours × 52 weeks).\n\n**Salary to hourly:** Divide annual salary by 2,080  \n**Hourly to salary:** Multiply hourly rate by 2,080\n\n| Annual Salary | Hourly Equivalent (40 hrs/wk) |\n|---|---|\n| $31,200 | $15.00/hr |\n| $41,600 | $20.00/hr |\n| $52,000 | $25.00/hr |\n| $62,400 | $30.00/hr |\n| $83,200 | $40.00/hr |\n| $104,000 | $50.00/hr |\n\nThe [hourly to salary calculator](/calculators/hourly-to-salary-calculator) handles this in both directions, including part-time schedules.\n\n## The Overtime Factor: When Hourly Wins\n\nUnder the federal Fair Labor Standards Act (FLSA), most hourly workers are **non-exempt** and must receive 1.5× pay for overtime. Salaried workers classified as **exempt** typically receive no overtime regardless of hours worked.\n\nThis matters enormously in practice:\n\n**Example — 50-hour work weeks:**\n\n| Role | Annual Salary / Rate | Regular Hours | OT Hours | Annual Take-Home (pre-tax) |\n|---|---|---|---|---|\n| Salaried employee | $52,000/yr | 50 hrs/wk | 0 paid | $52,000 |\n| Hourly at equiv. rate | $25.00/hr | 40 hrs/wk | 10 hrs/wk OT | $52,000 + $19,500 OT = **$71,500** |\n\nThe hourly worker earns 37% more in this scenario simply because overtime is compensated. If you regularly work more than 40 hours, hourly pay with overtime can significantly outperform an equivalent salary.\n\nUse the [paycheck calculator](/calculators/paycheck-calculator) to model your actual after-tax take-home at different wage structures.\n\n## Benefits: The Hidden Salary Premium\n\nSalaried positions more commonly include:\n- **Health insurance** (employer-subsidized)\n- **Paid time off (PTO)** and sick leave\n- **401(k) matching** (typically 3–6% of salary)\n- **Paid holidays** (typically 10–11 per year)\n- **Disability and life insurance**\n\nThe dollar value of benefits is substantial. Employer-sponsored health insurance averages roughly $7,000–$8,000 per year for individual coverage. A 401(k) match of 4% on a $52,000 salary is $2,080/year in additional compensation. These perks can add $10,000–$20,000 of real value on top of base pay.\n\n<div class=\"callout\">\nWhen comparing a $52,000 salary with full benefits vs a $28/hr freelance or hourly rate with no benefits, the salaried position may actually be worth more in total compensation — even though the hourly math ($28 × 2,080 = $58,240) looks higher.\n</div>\n\n## Salary vs Hourly: Which Is Better for Taxes?\n\nBoth salaried and hourly workers pay the same **federal and state income taxes** on their earnings. The IRS doesn't distinguish between pay structures — what matters is total taxable income for the year.\n\nThe practical difference is **withholding predictability**: salaried workers have the same withholding every paycheck, making budgeting straightforward. Hourly workers with variable hours may see bigger swings in withholding from check to check.\n\nNeither structure lets you avoid FICA taxes (Social Security 6.2% + Medicare 1.45%, up to the annual wage base).\n\n## When Salary Makes More Sense\n\n**Choose salary when:**\n- Your actual hours are consistently under 50/week\n- The benefits package (health, 401k, PTO) is strong\n- Job security and predictable income matter more than upside\n- You're in a professional or managerial role where promotion tracks are well-defined\n- The role requires travel, after-hours calls, or irregular schedules that would rack up overtime billing otherwise\n\n## When Hourly Makes More Sense\n\n**Choose hourly when:**\n- You routinely work overtime and it will be compensated\n- You value the option to pick up extra hours (or reduce them) without a fixed commitment\n- You're building a career in a skilled trade, healthcare, or any field where hourly rates are high\n- You plan to freelance or consult alongside the role\n- The employer offers no meaningful benefits with the salaried option\n\n## Real-World Example: Nurse vs. Salaried Healthcare Administrator\n\nA registered nurse earning $40/hr working 48 hours a week earns:\n- Regular: 40 hrs × $40 = $1,600/week\n- Overtime: 8 hrs × $60 = $480/week\n- **Weekly total: $2,080 / Annual: ~$108,160**\n\nA salaried healthcare administrator at $90,000/year working the same 48 hours earns:\n- **Annual: $90,000** — no overtime, regardless of hours\n\nThe nurse earns $18,000 more per year despite a lower \"base\" because overtime is paid. The administrator trades that for predictability, PTO, and potentially higher-level benefits.\n\n## Frequently Asked Questions\n\n**Can a salaried employee be forced to work unlimited hours?**\nIf you're classified as an **exempt** salaried employee under the FLSA, yes — your employer can require more than 40 hours with no additional pay. Exemption thresholds apply (as of 2024, the federal threshold is $684/week or $35,568/year to be exempt). Some states have higher thresholds.\n\n**Can hourly employees receive benefits?**\nYes. Many employers offer benefits to full-time hourly employees (those working 30+ hours/week). The Affordable Care Act requires employers with 50+ full-time-equivalent employees to offer coverage to workers averaging 30+ hours/week.\n\n**Does salary always come with more job security?**\nNot necessarily. Salaried employees can be laid off too, often without overtime pay during the notice period. However, salaried roles do tend to come with more formal review processes and defined roles that are slightly harder to eliminate.\n\n**How do I negotiate a salary vs hourly offer?**\nCompare total compensation, not just the headline number. Add up the value of benefits, expected hours, overtime potential, and career trajectory. Use the [salary to hourly calculator](/calculators/salary-to-hourly-calculator) to put both options on an equal footing before the conversation.\n\n**Is a $25/hour job better than a $50,000 salary?**\nAt 40 hours/week, $25/hr = $52,000/year — slightly better. But if the salaried role has health insurance, 401k matching, and PTO worth $12,000+, the $50,000 salary has equal or greater total value. It depends on the benefits and hours.\n\n## The Bottom Line\n\nSalary and hourly pay each have clear advantages depending on your hours, industry, and priorities. Hourly wins when overtime is a reality and benefits aren't on the table. Salary wins when total compensation (including benefits) is competitive and consistent hours keep your effective hourly rate high.\n\nBefore accepting any offer, run the numbers: use the [salary to hourly calculator](/calculators/salary-to-hourly-calculator) to see the hourly equivalent, and the [paycheck calculator](/calculators/paycheck-calculator) to estimate your real take-home after taxes and deductions.\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"stop_details":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":1,"cache_creation_input_tokens":364,"cache_read_input_tokens":83967,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":364,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0},"output_tokens":39,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"}},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"1c8a1dfc-fb9e-493f-8684-48d48c0fa434","uuid":"bb8578c7-6131-4389-b160-d3658f21b50e"}
